<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home,_Heriot_Row.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg/170px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg/255px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg/340px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home%2C_Heriot_Row.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_childhood_home,_Heriot_Row.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson's childhood home in Heriot Row</div></div></div>
<p>Stevenson was born at 8&#160;Howard Place, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland, on 13 November 1850, to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Stevenson" title="Thomas Stevenson">Thomas Stevenson</a> (1818–87), a leading <a href="/wiki/Lighthouse" title="Lighthouse">lighthouse</a> engineer, and his wife Margaret Isabella (born Balfour; 1829–97). He was christened Robert Lewis Balfour Stevenson. At about age 18, Stevenson changed the spelling of "Lewis" to "Louis", and in 1873, he dropped "Balfour".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lighthouse design was the family's profession: Thomas's father (Robert's grandfather) was the famous civil engineer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stevenson_(civil_engineer)" title="Robert Stevenson (civil engineer)">Robert Stevenson</a>, and both of Thomas's brothers (Robert's uncles) <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stevenson" title="Alan Stevenson">Alan</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Stevenson_(engineer)" title="David Stevenson (engineer)">David</a>, were in the same field.<sup id="cite_ref-Paxton_2004_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paxton_2004-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, even Thomas's maternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Smith_(engineer)" title="Thomas Smith (engineer)">Thomas Smith</a>, had been in the same profession. However, Robert's mother's family were not of the same profession. Margaret's natal family, the Balfours, were <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a>, tracing their lineage back to a certain Alexander Balfour who had held the lands of <a href="/wiki/Inchyra" title="Inchyra">Inchyra</a> in Fife in the fifteenth century. Margaret's father, Lewis Balfour (1777–1860), was a <a href="/wiki/Minister_(Christianity)" title="Minister (Christianity)">minister</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> at nearby <a href="/wiki/Colinton" title="Colinton">Colinton</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and her siblings included the physician <a href="/wiki/George_William_Balfour" title="George William Balfour">George William Balfour</a> and the marine engineer <a href="/wiki/James_Balfour_(engineer)" title="James Balfour (engineer)">James Balfour</a>. Stevenson spent the greater part of his boyhood holidays in his maternal grandfather's house. "Now I often wonder," wrote Stevenson, "what I inherited from this old minister. I must suppose, indeed, that he was fond of preaching <a href="/wiki/Sermon" title="Sermon">sermons</a>, and so am I, though I never heard it maintained that either of us loved to hear them."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Lewis Balfour and his daughter both had weak chests, so they often needed to stay in warmer climates for their health. Stevenson inherited a tendency to coughs and fevers, exacerbated when the family moved to a damp, chilly house at 1&#160;Inverleith Terrace in&#160;1851.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The family moved again to the sunnier 17&#160;Heriot Row when Stevenson was six years old, but the tendency to extreme sickness in winter remained with him until he was eleven. Illness would be a recurrent feature of his adult life and left him extraordinarily thin.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Contemporary views were that he had <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, but more recent views are that it was <a href="/wiki/Bronchiectasis" title="Bronchiectasis">bronchiectasis</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> or even <a href="/wiki/Sarcoidosis" title="Sarcoidosis">sarcoidosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Stevenson's parents were both devout and serious <a href="/wiki/Presbyterians" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterians">Presbyterians</a>, but the household was not strict in its adherence to Calvinist principles. His nurse, Alison Cunningham (known as Cummy),<sup id="cite_ref-NYT1913_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT1913-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> was more fervently religious. Her <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a> and folk beliefs were an early source of nightmares for the child, and he showed a precocious concern for religion.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> But she also cared for him tenderly in illness, reading to him from <a href="/wiki/John_Bunyan" title="John Bunyan">Bunyan</a> and the Bible as he lay sick in bed and telling tales of the <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a>. Stevenson recalled this time of sickness in "The Land of Counterpane" in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_Garden_of_Verses" title="A Child&#39;s Garden of Verses">A Child's Garden of Verses</a></i> (1885),<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> dedicating the book to his nurse.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg/170px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg" width="170" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg/255px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg 2x" data-file-width="327" data-file-height="453" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_mit_7_Jahren.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Louis Stevenson at the age of seven</div></div></div>
<p>An only child, strange-looking and eccentric, Stevenson found it hard to fit in when he was sent to a nearby school at age six, a problem repeated at age eleven when he went on to the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Academy" title="Edinburgh Academy">Edinburgh Academy</a>; but he mixed well in lively games with his cousins in summer holidays at <a href="/wiki/Colinton" title="Colinton">Colinton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In any case, his frequent illnesses often kept him away from his first school, so he was taught for long stretches by private tutors. He was a late reader, first learning at age seven or eight, but even before this he dictated stories to his mother and nurse.<sup id="cite_ref-Mehew_2004_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mehew_2004-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> He compulsively wrote stories throughout his childhood. His father was proud of this interest; he had also written stories in his spare time until his own father found them and told him to "give up such nonsense and mind your business."<sup id="cite_ref-Paxton_2004_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paxton_2004-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> He paid for the printing of Robert's first publication at sixteen, an account of the <a href="/wiki/Covenanters#Restoration_and_the_&quot;Killing_Time&quot;" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenanters">Covenanters' rebellion</a>, which was published on its two hundredth anniversary, <i>The Pentland Rising: A Page of History, 1666</i> (1866).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 1857, Stevenson went to <i>Mr Henderson's School</i> in India Street, Edinburgh, but because of poor health stayed only a few weeks and did not return until October 1859. During his many absences he was taught by private tutors. In October 1861, he went to <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Academy" title="Edinburgh Academy">Edinburgh Academy</a>, an independent school for boys, and stayed there sporadically for about fifteen months. In the autumn of 1863, he spent one term at an English boarding school at Spring Grove in <a href="/wiki/Isleworth" title="Isleworth">Isleworth</a> in Middlesex (now an urban area of West London). In October 1864, following an improvement to his health, he was sent to Robert Thomson's private school in Frederick Street, Edinburgh, where he remained until he went to university.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In November 1867, Stevenson entered the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a> to study engineering. He showed from the start no enthusiasm for his studies and devoted much energy to avoiding lectures. This time was more important for the friendships he made with other students in the <a href="/wiki/The_Speculative_Society" title="The Speculative Society">Speculative Society</a> (an exclusive debating club), particularly with Charles Baxter, who would become Stevenson's financial agent, and with a professor, <a href="/wiki/Fleeming_Jenkin" title="Fleeming Jenkin">Fleeming Jenkin</a>, whose house staged amateur drama in which Stevenson took part, and whose biography he would later write.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps most important at this point in his life was a cousin, Robert Alan Mowbray Stevenson (known as "Bob"), a lively and light-hearted young man who, instead of the family profession, had chosen to study art.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Each year during vacations, Stevenson travelled to inspect the family's engineering works—to <a href="/wiki/Anstruther" title="Anstruther">Anstruther</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wick,_Highland" class="mw-redirect" title="Wick, Highland">Wick</a> in 1868, with his father on his official tour of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> islands lighthouses in 1869, and for three weeks to the island of <a href="/wiki/Erraid" title="Erraid">Erraid</a> in 1870. He enjoyed the travels more for the material they gave for his writing than for any engineering interest. The voyage with his father pleased him because a similar journey of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a> with Robert Stevenson had provided the inspiration for Scott's 1822 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pirate_(novel)" title="The Pirate (novel)">The Pirate</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In April 1871, Stevenson notified his father of his decision to pursue a life of letters. Though the elder Stevenson was naturally disappointed, the surprise cannot have been great, and Stevenson's mother reported that he was "wonderfully resigned" to his son's choice. To provide some security, it was agreed that Stevenson should read Law (again at Edinburgh University) and <a href="/wiki/Admission_to_practice_law" title="Admission to practice law">be called to the Scottish bar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In his 1887 poetry collection <i>Underwoods</i>, Stevenson muses on his having turned from the family profession:<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Say not of me that weakly I declined<br />
The labours of my sires, and fled the sea,<br />
The towers we founded and the lamps we lit,<br />
To play at home with paper like a child.<br />
But rather say: <i>In the afternoon of time</i><br />
<i>A strenuous family dusted from its hands</i><br />
<i>The sand of granite, and beholding far</i><br />
<i>Along the sounding coast its pyramids</i><br />
<i>And tall memorials catch the dying sun,</i><br />
<i>Smiled well content, and to this childish task</i><br />
<i>Around the fire addressed its evening hours.</i>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In other respects too, Stevenson was moving away from his upbringing. His dress became more <a href="/wiki/Bohemianism" title="Bohemianism">Bohemian</a>; he already wore his hair long, but he now took to wearing a velveteen jacket and rarely attended parties in conventional evening dress.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Within the limits of a strict allowance, he visited cheap pubs and brothels.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> More importantly, he had come to reject Christianity and declared himself an atheist.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In January 1873, his father came across the constitution of the LJR (Liberty, Justice, Reverence) Club, of which Stevenson and his cousin Bob were members, which began: "Disregard everything our parents have taught us". Questioning his son about his beliefs, he discovered the truth, leading to a long period of dissension with both parents:<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>What a <i>damned</i> curse I am to my parents! As my father said "You have rendered my whole life a failure". As my mother said "This is the heaviest affliction that has ever befallen me". O Lord, what a pleasant thing it is to have damned the happiness of (probably) the only two people who care a damn about you in the world.
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_writing_and_travels">Early writing and travels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early writing and travels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg" width="220" height="303" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg 2x" data-file-width="376" data-file-height="518" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_at_26.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson c. 1877</div></div></div>
<p>Stevenson was visiting a cousin in England in late 1873 when he met two people who became very important to him—<a href="/wiki/Sidney_Colvin" title="Sidney Colvin">Sidney Colvin</a> and Fanny (Frances Jane) Sitwell. Sitwell was a 34-year-old woman with a son, who was separated from her husband. She attracted the devotion of many who met her, including Colvin, who eventually married her in 1901. Stevenson was also drawn to her, and they kept up a heated correspondence over several years in which he wavered between the role of a suitor and a son (he addressed her as "Madonna").<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Colvin became Stevenson's literary adviser and was the first editor of Stevenson's letters after his death. Soon after their first meeting, he had placed Stevenson's first paid contribution in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Portfolio" title="The Portfolio">The Portfolio</a></i>—an essay titled "Roads".<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Stevenson was soon active in London literary life, becoming acquainted with many of the writers of the time, including <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lang" title="Andrew Lang">Andrew Lang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gosse" title="Edmund Gosse">Edmund Gosse</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Stephen" title="Leslie Stephen">Leslie Stephen</a>, the editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cornhill_Magazine" title="Cornhill Magazine">Cornhill Magazine</a></i> who took an interest in Stevenson's work. Stephen in turn introduced him to a more important friend while visiting Edinburgh in 1875. He took Stevenson with him to visit a patient at the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Infirmary" class="mw-redirect" title="Edinburgh Infirmary">Edinburgh Infirmary</a> named <a href="/wiki/William_Ernest_Henley" title="William Ernest Henley">William Ernest Henley</a>, an energetic and talkative man with a wooden leg. Henley became a close friend and occasional literary collaborator, until a quarrel broke up the friendship in 1888. Henley is often seen as the model for Long John Silver in <i>Treasure Island</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Stevenson was sent to <a href="/wiki/Menton" title="Menton">Menton</a> on the <a href="/wiki/French_Riviera" title="French Riviera">French Riviera</a> in November 1873 to recuperate after his health failed. He returned in better health in April 1874 and settled down to his studies, but he returned to France several times after that.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> He made long and frequent trips to the neighborhood of the <a href="/wiki/Forest_of_Fontainebleau" title="Forest of Fontainebleau">Forest of Fontainebleau</a>, staying at <a href="/wiki/Barbizon" title="Barbizon">Barbizon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grez-sur-Loing" title="Grez-sur-Loing">Grez-sur-Loing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nemours" title="Nemours">Nemours</a> and becoming a member of the artists' colonies there, as well as to Paris to visit galleries and the theatres.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He qualified for the Scottish bar in July 1875, and his father added a brass plate to the Heriot Row house reading "R.L. Stevenson, Advocate". His law studies did influence his books, but he never practised law;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> all his energies were spent in travel and writing. One of his journeys was a canoe voyage in Belgium and France with Sir Walter Simpson, a friend from the Speculative Society, a frequent travel companion, and the author of <i>The Art of Golf</i> (1887). This trip was the basis of his first travel book <i><a href="/wiki/An_Inland_Voyage" title="An Inland Voyage">An Inland Voyage</a></i> (1878).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg/170px-Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg/255px-Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg/340px-Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="568" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fanny_Osbourne_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fanny Van de Grift Osbourne, c. 1876</div></div></div>
<p>The canoe voyage with Simpson brought Stevenson to Grez in September 1876, where he first met <a href="/wiki/Fanny_Van_de_Grift_Osbourne" class="mw-redirect" title="Fanny Van de Grift Osbourne">Fanny Van de Grift Osbourne</a> (1840–1914). Born in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a>, she had married at age seventeen and moved to Nevada to rejoin husband Samuel after his participation in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. That marriage produced three children: <a href="/wiki/Isobel_Osbourne" title="Isobel Osbourne">Isobel</a> (or "Belle"), <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Osbourne" title="Lloyd Osbourne">Lloyd</a>, and Hervey (who died in 1875). But anger over her husband's infidelities led to a number of separations. In 1875, she had taken her children to France, where she and Isobel studied art.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Stevenson returned to Britain shortly after this first meeting, Fanny apparently remained in his thoughts, and he wrote an essay, "On falling in love", for the <i>Cornhill Magazine</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> They met again early in 1877 and became lovers. Stevenson spent much of the following year with her and her children in France.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In August 1878, Fanny returned to San Francisco, California. Stevenson at first remained in Europe, making the walking trip that would form the basis for <i><a href="/wiki/Travels_with_a_Donkey_in_the_C%C3%A9vennes" title="Travels with a Donkey in the Cévennes">Travels with a Donkey in the Cévennes</a></i> (1879). But in August 1879, he set off to join her, against the advice of his friends and without notifying his parents. He took second-class passage on the steamship <i><a href="/wiki/Devonia_(steamer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Devonia (steamer)">Devonia</a></i>, in part to save money but also to learn how others traveled and to increase the adventure of the journey.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> From New York City, he traveled overland by train to California. He later wrote about the experience in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amateur_Emigrant" title="The Amateur Emigrant">The Amateur Emigrant</a></i>. Although it was good experience for his literature, it broke his health. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House,_530_Houston_Street,_Monterey_(Monterey_County,_California).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House%2C_530_Houston_Street%2C_Monterey_%28Monterey_County%2C_California%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="562" data-file-height="348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_House,_530_Houston_Street,_Monterey_(Monterey_County,_California).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French Hotel (now "<a href="/wiki/Monterey_State_Historic_Park#Stevenson_House" title="Monterey State Historic Park">Stevenson House</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>, where he stayed in 1879</div></div></div>
<p>He was near death when he arrived in <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>, where some local <a href="/wiki/Ranch" title="Ranch">ranchers</a> nursed him back to health. In Monterey he stayed for a time at the French Hotel (located at 530 Houston Street), now called the "<a href="/wiki/Monterey_State_Historic_Park#Stevenson_House" title="Monterey State Historic Park">Stevenson House</a>" after him and now a museum dedicated to his memory. While there, he often dined "on the cuff," as he said, at a nearby restaurant run by a Frenchman, Jules Simoneau, that stood at what is now Simoneau Plaza; several years later, he sent Simoneau an inscribed copy of his novel <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Case_of_Dr_Jekyll_and_Mr_Hyde" title="Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde">Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde</a></i> (1886), writing that it would be a stranger case still if Robert Louis Stevenson ever forgot Jules Simoneau. While in Monterey, Stevenson wrote an evocative article about "the Old Pacific Capital", Monterey.
</p><p>By December 1879, Stevenson had recovered his health enough to continue to San Francisco, where for several months he struggled "all alone on forty-five cents a day, and sometimes less, with quantities of hard work and many heavy thoughts,"<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> in an effort to support himself through his writing. But by the end of the winter, his health was broken again and he found himself at death's door. Fanny, now divorced and recovered from her own illness, came to Stevenson's bedside and nursed him to recovery. "After a while," he wrote, "my spirit got up again in a divine frenzy, and has since kicked and spurred my vile body forward with great emphasis and success."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> When his father heard of his condition, he cabled him money to help him through this period.
</p><p>Fanny and Robert were married in May 1880, although, as he said, he was "a mere complication of cough and bones, much fitter for an emblem of mortality than a bridegroom."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> With his new wife and her son, Lloyd,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> he travelled north of San Francisco to <a href="/wiki/Napa_County,_California" title="Napa County, California">Napa Valley</a> and spent a summer honeymoon at an abandoned mining camp on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Saint_Helena" title="Mount Saint Helena">Mount Saint Helena</a>. He wrote about this experience in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Silverado_Squatters" title="The Silverado Squatters">The Silverado Squatters</a></i>. He met <a href="/wiki/Charles_Warren_Stoddard" title="Charles Warren Stoddard">Charles Warren Stoddard</a>, co-editor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Overland_Monthly" title="Overland Monthly">Overland Monthly</a></i> and author of <i>South Sea Idylls</i>, who urged Stevenson to travel to the South Pacific, an idea which would return to him many years later. In August 1880, he sailed with Fanny and Lloyd from New York to Britain and found his parents and his friend <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Colvin" title="Sidney Colvin">Sidney Colvin</a> on the wharf at <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, happy to see him return home. Gradually, his new wife was able to patch up differences between father and son and make herself a part of the new family through her charm and wit.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempted_settlement_in_Europe_and_the_US">Attempted settlement in Europe and the US</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Attempted settlement in Europe and the US">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG/220px-StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG/330px-StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG/440px-StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1607" data-file-height="894" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StevensonAdirondackHome.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson's "<a href="/wiki/Cure_Cottages_of_Saranac_Lake" title="Cure Cottages of Saranac Lake">Cure Cottage</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Saranac_Lake,_New_York" title="Saranac Lake, New York">Saranac Lake</a></div></div></div>
<p>For the next seven years, between 1880 and 1887, Stevenson searched in vain for a place of residence suitable to his state of health. He spent his summers at various places in Scotland and England, including <a href="/wiki/Westbourne,_Dorset" title="Westbourne, Dorset">Westbourne, Dorset</a>, a residential area in <a href="/wiki/Bournemouth" title="Bournemouth">Bournemouth</a>. It was during his time in Bournemouth that he wrote the story <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Case_of_Dr_Jekyll_and_Mr_Hyde" title="Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde">Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde</a></i>, naming one of the characters (Mr Poole) after the town of <a href="/wiki/Poole" title="Poole">Poole</a>, which is situated next to Bournemouth. In Westbourne he named his house <i><a href="/wiki/Skerryvore" title="Skerryvore">Skerryvore</a></i> after the tallest lighthouse in Scotland, which his uncle <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stevenson" title="Alan Stevenson">Alan</a> had built (1838–1844). In the wintertime, Stevenson travelled to France and lived at <a href="/wiki/Davos_Platz" class="mw-redirect" title="Davos Platz">Davos Platz</a><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and the Chalet de Solitude at <a href="/wiki/Hy%C3%A8res" title="Hyères">Hyères</a>, where, for a time, he enjoyed almost complete happiness. "I have so many things to make life sweet for me," he wrote, "it seems a pity I cannot have that other one thing—health. But though you will be angry to hear it, I believe, for myself at least, what is is best. I believed it all through my worst days, and I am not ashamed to profess it now."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> In spite of his ill health, he produced the bulk of his best-known work during these years: <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i>, which was published under the pseudonym "Captain George North" became his first widely popular book; <i><a href="/wiki/Kidnapped_(novel)" title="Kidnapped (novel)">Kidnapped</a></i>; <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Case_of_Dr_Jekyll_and_Mr_Hyde" title="Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde">Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde</a></i>, the story which established his wider reputation; <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Arrow:_A_Tale_of_the_Two_Roses" title="The Black Arrow: A Tale of the Two Roses">The Black Arrow: A Tale of the Two Roses</a></i>; and two volumes of verse, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Child%27s_Garden_of_Verses" title="A Child&#39;s Garden of Verses">A Child's Garden of Verses</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Underwoods" title="Underwoods">Underwoods</a></i>. At Skerryvore he gave a copy of <i>Kidnapped</i> to his friend and frequent visitor <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When his father died in 1887, Stevenson felt free to follow the advice of his physician to try a complete change of climate, and he started with his mother and family for <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>. But after landing in New York, they decided to spend the winter at <a href="/wiki/Saranac_Lake,_New_York" title="Saranac Lake, New York">Saranac Lake, New York</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Adirondacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Adirondacks">Adirondacks</a> at a cure cottage now known as <a href="/wiki/Stevenson_Cottage" title="Stevenson Cottage">Stevenson Cottage</a>. During the intensely cold winter, Stevenson wrote some of his best essays, including <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pulvis_et_Umbra&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pulvis et Umbra (page does not exist)">Pulvis et Umbra</a></i>, began <i><a href="/wiki/The_Master_of_Ballantrae" title="The Master of Ballantrae">The Master of Ballantrae</a></i>, and lightheartedly planned, for the following summer, a cruise to the southern Pacific Ocean. "The proudest moments of my life," he wrote, "have been passed in the stern-sheets of a boat with that romantic garment over my shoulders."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Much like his father, Stevenson believed in <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">Conservatism</a> for most of his life. His cousin and biographer, <a href="/wiki/Sir_Graham_Balfour#Links_with_RLS" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Graham Balfour">Sir Graham Balfour</a>, said that "he probably throughout life would, if compelled to vote, have always supported the Conservative candidate."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1866, Stevenson voted for <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, future Conservative <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a>, over <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>, for the role of Lord Rectorship of the University of Edinburgh.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> During his college years, he briefly identified himself as a "red-hot socialist". By 1877, at only twenty-six years of age and before having written most of his major fictional works, Stevenson reflected: "For my part, I look back to the time when I was a Socialist with something like regret. I have convinced myself (for the moment) that we had better leave these great changes to what we call great blind forces: their blindness being so much more perspicacious than the little, peering, partial eyesight of men [...] Now I know that in thus turning Conservative with years, I am going through the normal cycle of change and travelling in the common orbit of men's opinions. I submit to this, as I would submit to gout or gray hair, as a concomitant of growing age or else of failing animal heat; but I do not acknowledge that it is necessarily a change for the better—I dare say it is deplorably for the worse."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Journey_to_the_Pacific">Journey to the Pacific</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Journey to the Pacific">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg 2x" data-file-width="459" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_King_Kalakaua_and_his_Singing_Boys.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson and King <a href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81kaua" title="Kalākaua">Kalākaua</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, c. 1889</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stevenson_vailima.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Stevenson_vailima.jpg/220px-Stevenson_vailima.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Stevenson_vailima.jpg/330px-Stevenson_vailima.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Stevenson_vailima.jpg/440px-Stevenson_vailima.jpg 2x" data-file-width="602" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stevenson_vailima.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The author with his wife and their household in <a href="/wiki/Vailima,_Samoa" title="Vailima, Samoa">Vailima, Samoa</a>, c. 1892</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1400" data-file-height="1093" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_and_family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Louis Stevenson and family 1893</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete,_Samoa_1896.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete%2C_Samoa_1896.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2158" data-file-height="1470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_birthday_fete,_Samoa_1896.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson's birthday fete at Vailima, Nov. 1894</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg/220px-Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg/330px-Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg/440px-Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2541" data-file-height="1502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stevenson%27s_home_at_Vailima.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson's home at <a href="/wiki/Vailima,_Samoa" title="Vailima, Samoa">Vailima, Samoa</a> showing him on the veranda c.1893</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa,_1894.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg/220px-Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg/330px-Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg/440px-Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa%2C_1894.jpg 2x" data-file-width="621" data-file-height="467" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burial_and_grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_in_Samoa,_1894.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burial on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vaea" title="Mount Vaea">Mount Vaea</a> in Samoa, 1894</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg/220px-Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg/330px-Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg/440px-Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg 2x" data-file-width="589" data-file-height="359" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Robert_Louis_Stevenson_1909.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson's tomb on Mt. Vaea, c. 1909</div></div></div>
<p>In June 1888, Stevenson chartered the yacht <i>Casco</i> and set sail with his family from San Francisco. The vessel "plowed her path of snow across the empty deep, far from all track of commerce, far from any hand of help."<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The sea air and thrill of adventure for a time restored his health, and for nearly three years he wandered the eastern and central Pacific, stopping for extended stays at the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaiian Islands</a>, where he spent much time with and became a good friend of King <a href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81kaua" title="Kalākaua">Kalākaua</a>. He befriended the king's niece, Princess <a href="/wiki/Ka%27iulani" class="mw-redirect" title="Ka&#39;iulani">Victoria Kaiulani</a>, who also had a link to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish heritage</a>. He spent time at the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Islands" title="Gilbert Islands">Gilbert Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a>, New Zealand, and the <a href="/wiki/Samoan_Islands" title="Samoan Islands">Samoan Islands</a>. During this period, he completed <i><a href="/wiki/The_Master_of_Ballantrae" title="The Master of Ballantrae">The Master of Ballantrae</a></i>, composed two ballads based on the legends of the islanders, and wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bottle_Imp" title="The Bottle Imp">The Bottle Imp</a></i>. He witnessed the <a href="/wiki/Samoan_crisis" title="Samoan crisis">Samoan crisis</a>. He preserved the experience of these years in his various letters and in his <i>In the South Seas</i> (which was published posthumously),<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> an account of the 1888 cruise which Stevenson and Fanny undertook on the <i>Casco</i> from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaiian Islands</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Marquesas" class="mw-redirect" title="Marquesas">Marquesas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuamotu" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuamotu">Tuamotu</a> islands. An 1889 voyage, this time with Lloyd, on the trading schooner <i><a href="/wiki/Equator_(schooner)" title="Equator (schooner)">Equator</a></i>, visiting <a href="/wiki/Butaritari" title="Butaritari">Butaritari</a>, Mariki, Apaiang, and <a href="/wiki/Abemama" title="Abemama">Abemama</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Gilbert Islands</a>, (also known as the Kingsmills) now <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Seas_1896_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Seas_1896-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> During the 1889 voyage, they spent several months on <a href="/wiki/Abemama" title="Abemama">Abemama</a> with the tyrant-chief <a href="/wiki/Tem_Binoka" class="mw-redirect" title="Tem Binoka">Tem Binoka</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Abemama" title="Abemama">Abemama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aranuka" title="Aranuka">Aranuka</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kuria_(islands)" title="Kuria (islands)">Kuria</a>. Stevenson extensively described Binoka in <i>In the South Seas</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-South_Seas_1896_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-South_Seas_1896-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One particular <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a> from this period stands as testimony to his activism and indignation at the pettiness of the "powers that be", in the person of a Presbyterian minister in Honolulu called <a href="/wiki/Charles_McEwen_Hyde" title="Charles McEwen Hyde">Charles McEwen Hyde</a>. During his time in the Hawaiian Islands, Stevenson had visited <a href="/wiki/Molokai" title="Molokai">Molokai</a> and the leper colony there, shortly after the demise of <a href="/wiki/Father_Damien" title="Father Damien">Father Damien</a>. When Dr. Hyde wrote a letter to a fellow clergyman speaking ill of Father Damien, Stevenson wrote a scathing open letter of rebuke to Dr. Hyde.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Soon afterwards, in April 1890, Stevenson left Sydney on the <i>Janet Nicoll</i> for his third and final voyage among the South Seas islands.<sup id="cite_ref-fanny1914_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fanny1914-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Stevenson intended to produce another book of travel writing to follow his earlier book <i>In the South Seas</i>, it was his wife who eventually published her journal of their third voyage. (Fanny misnames the ship as the <i>Janet Nichol</i> in her account of the 1890 voyage, <i>The Cruise of the Janet Nichol</i>.)<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> A fellow passenger was <a href="/wiki/Jack_Buckland" title="Jack Buckland">Jack Buckland</a>, whose stories of life as an island trader became the inspiration for the character of Tommy Hadden in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wrecker_(Stevenson_novel)" title="The Wrecker (Stevenson novel)">The Wrecker</a></i> (1892), which Stevenson and <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Osbourne" title="Lloyd Osbourne">Lloyd Osbourne</a> wrote together.<sup id="cite_ref-Jack_Buckland_2001_p._418_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jack_Buckland_2001_p._418-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Buckland visited the Stevensons at <a href="/wiki/Vailima,_Samoa" title="Vailima, Samoa">Vailima</a> in 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years">Last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1890, Stevenson purchased a tract of about 400 acres (1.6&#160;km²) in <a href="/wiki/Upolu" title="Upolu">Upolu</a>, an island in <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>. Here, after two aborted attempts to visit Scotland, he established himself, after much work, upon his estate in the village of <a href="/wiki/Vailima,_Samoa" title="Vailima, Samoa">Vailima</a>. He took the native name Tusitala (<a href="/wiki/Samoan_language" title="Samoan language">Samoan</a> for "Teller of Tales", i.e. a storyteller). His influence spread to the Samoans, who consulted him for advice, and he soon became involved in local politics. He was convinced the European officials appointed to rule the Samoans were incompetent, and after many futile attempts to resolve the matter, he published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Footnote_to_History:_Eight_Years_of_Trouble_in_Samoa" title="A Footnote to History: Eight Years of Trouble in Samoa">A Footnote to History</a></i>. This was such a stinging protest against existing conditions that it resulted in the recall of two officials, and Stevenson feared for a time it would result in his own deportation. When things had finally blown over he wrote to Colvin, who came from a family of distinguished colonial administrators, "I used to think meanly of the plumber; but how he shines beside the politician!"<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Stevensons were on friendly terms with some of the colonial leaders and their families. At one point, he formally donated, by <a href="/wiki/Deed" title="Deed">deed</a> of gift, his birthday to the daughter of the American Land Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay_Ide" title="Henry Clay Ide">Henry Clay Ide</a> since she was born on Christmas Day and had no birthday celebration separate from the family's Christmas celebrations. This led to a strong bond between the Stevenson and Ide families.<sup id="cite_ref-Gauthier_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gauthier-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Colley_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Colley-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to building his house and clearing his land and helping the Samoans in many ways, he found time to work at his writing. He felt that "there was never any man had so many irons in the fire".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> He wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Island_Nights%27_Entertainments" title="Island Nights&#39; Entertainments">The Beach of Falesa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Catriona_(novel)" title="Catriona (novel)">Catriona</a></i> (titled <i>David Balfour</i> in the US),<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ebb-Tide" title="The Ebb-Tide">The Ebb-Tide</a></i>, and the <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Vailima_Letters&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vailima Letters (page does not exist)">Vailima Letters</a></i> during this period.
</p><p>Stevenson grew depressed and wondered if he had exhausted his creative vein as he had been "overworked bitterly".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> He felt that with each fresh attempt, the best he could write was "ditch-water".<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He even feared that he might again become a helpless invalid. He rebelled against this idea: "I wish to die in my boots; no more Land of Counterpane for me. To be drowned, to be shot, to be thrown from a horse — ay, to be hanged, rather than pass again through that slow dissolution."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> He then suddenly had a return of his old energy and he began work on <i><a href="/wiki/Weir_of_Hermiston" title="Weir of Hermiston">Weir of Hermiston</a></i>. "It's so good that it frightens me," he is reported to have exclaimed.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> He felt that this was the best work he had done. He was convinced, "sick and well, I have had splendid life of it, grudge nothing, regret very little ... take it all over, damnation and all, would hardly change with any man of my time."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 3 December 1894, Stevenson was talking to his wife and straining to open a bottle of wine when he suddenly exclaimed, "What's that!", asked his wife "Does my face look strange?" and collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He died within a few hours, probably of a <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_haemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Cerebral haemorrhage">cerebral haemorrhage</a>. He was forty-four years old. The Samoans insisted on surrounding his body with a watch-guard during the night and on bearing their Tusitala upon their shoulders to nearby <a href="/wiki/Mount_Vaea" title="Mount Vaea">Mount Vaea</a>, where they buried him on a spot overlooking the sea on land donated by British Acting Vice Consul <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Trood" title="Thomas Trood">Thomas Trood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Stevenson had always wanted his 'Requiem' inscribed on his tomb:<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><i>Under the wide and starry sky,</i></dd>
<dd><i>Dig the grave and let me lie.</i></dd>
<dd><i>Glad did I live and gladly die,</i></dd>
<dd><i>And I laid me down with a will.</i></dd>
<dd><i>This be the verse you grave for me:</i></dd>
<dd><i>Here he lies where he longed to be;</i></dd>
<dd><i>Home is the sailor, home from sea,</i></dd>
<dd><i>And the hunter home from the hill.</i></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>However, the piece is misquoted in many places, with an extra "the", including on his tomb:
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><i>Home is the sailor, home from <b>the</b> sea,</i></dd>
<dd><i>And the hunter home from the hill.</i></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Stevenson was loved by the Samoans, and his tombstone epigraph was translated to a Samoan song of grief<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> which is well-known and still sung in Samoa.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manuscripts">Manuscripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Manuscripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Half of Stevenson's original manuscripts are lost, including those of <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Arrow" class="mw-redirect" title="The Black Arrow">The Black Arrow</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Master_of_Ballantrae" title="The Master of Ballantrae">The Master of Ballantrae</a></i>. Stevenson's heirs sold Stevenson's papers during World War I; many Stevenson documents were auctioned off in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_compositions">Musical compositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Musical compositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Besides playing the <a href="/wiki/Piano" title="Piano">piano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flageolet" title="Flageolet">flageolet</a>, Stevenson wrote over 123 original musical compositions or arrangements, including solos, duets, trios and quartets for various combinations of flageolet, <a href="/wiki/Flute" title="Flute">flute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandolin" title="Mandolin">mandolin</a>, and piano. His works include ten songs written to his own poetry and with original or arranged melodies. In 1968, Robert Hughes arranged a number of Stevenson's works for chamber orchestra, which toured the Pacific Northwest that year.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_reception">Modern reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Modern reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rsl1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Rsl1.jpg/220px-Rsl1.jpg" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Rsl1.jpg/330px-Rsl1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/Rsl1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="430" data-file-height="519" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rsl1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stevenson in 1885</div></div></div>
<p>Stevenson was a celebrity in his own time, but with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Literary_modernism" title="Literary modernism">modernist literature</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, he was seen for much of the 20th century as a writer of the second class, relegated to children's literature and <a href="/wiki/Horror_fiction" title="Horror fiction">horror</a> <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genres</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arata_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arata-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Condemned by literary figures such as <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> (daughter of his early mentor <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Stephen" title="Leslie Stephen">Leslie Stephen</a>) and her husband <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Woolf" title="Leonard Woolf">Leonard</a>, he was gradually excluded from the canon of literature taught in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-arata_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arata-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> His exclusion reached a height when in the 1973 2,000-page <i>Oxford Anthology of English Literature</i> Stevenson was entirely unmentioned; and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Norton_Anthology_of_English_Literature" title="The Norton Anthology of English Literature">The Norton Anthology of English Literature</a></i> excluded him from 1968 to 2000 (1st–7th editions), including him only in the 8th edition (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-arata_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arata-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The late 20th century saw the start of a re-evaluation of Stevenson as an artist of great range and insight, a <a href="/wiki/Literary_theory" title="Literary theory">literary theorist</a>, an essayist and <a href="/wiki/Social_critic" class="mw-redirect" title="Social critic">social critic</a>, a witness to the colonial <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Pacific_Islands" title="History of the Pacific Islands">history of the Pacific Islands</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-arata_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arata-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Even as early as 1965, the pendulum had begun to swing: he was praised by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Lancelyn_Green" title="Roger Lancelyn Green">Roger Lancelyn Green</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> <a href="/wiki/Inklings" title="Inklings">Inklings</a>, as a writer of a consistently high level of "literary skill or sheer imaginative power" and a co-originator with <a href="/wiki/H._Rider_Haggard" title="H. Rider Haggard">H. Rider Haggard</a> of the Age of the Story Tellers.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He is now being re-evaluated as a peer of authors such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">Joseph Conrad</a> (whom Stevenson influenced with his South Seas fiction), and <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>, with new scholarly studies and organisations devoted to Stevenson.<sup id="cite_ref-arata_79-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arata-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the vicissitudes of his scholarly reception, Stevenson has remained popular worldwide. According to the <a href="/wiki/Index_Translationum" title="Index Translationum">Index Translationum</a>, Stevenson is ranked the 26th most translated author in the world, ahead of fellow nineteenth-century writers <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-indextrans_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indextrans-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monuments_and_commemoration">Monuments and commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Louis_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Monuments and commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG" width="220" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1245" data-file-height="1429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_head.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Head of Stevenson, Writer's Museum, Edinburgh</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_statue.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Robert Louis Stevenson as a child, outside Colinton Parish Church, Scotland</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Writers%27_Museum" title="Writers&#39; Museum">Writers' Museum</a> near Edinburgh's <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mile" title="Royal Mile">Royal Mile</a> devotes a room to Stevenson, containing some of his personal possessions from childhood through to adulthood.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Monterey_State_Historic_Park#Stevenson_House" title="Monterey State Historic Park">Stevenson House</a> at 530 Houston Street in <a href="/wiki/Monterey,_California" title="Monterey, California">Monterey, California</a>, formerly the French Hotel, memorializes Stevenson's 1879 stay in "the Old Pacific Capital", as he was crossing the United States to join his future wife, Fanny Osbourne. The Stevenson House museum is graced with a superb <a href="/wiki/Bas-relief" class="mw-redirect" title="Bas-relief">bas-relief</a> depicting the sickly author writing in bed.
</p><p>The Robert Louis Stevenson Museum in St. Helena, California, is home to over 11,000 objects and artifacts, the majority of which belonged to Stevenson. Opened in 1969, the museum houses such treasures as his childhood rocking chair, writing desk, toy soldiers, and personal writings among many other items. The museum is free to the public and serves as an academic archive for students, writers, and Stevenson enthusiasts. 
</p><p>Robert Louis Stevenson's former home in Vailima, Samoa is now a museum dedicated to the later years of his life. The museum collection includes several original items belonging to Stevenson and his family. The path to Stevenson's grave at the top of Mt Vaea commences from the museum.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg/220px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg/330px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg/440px-Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3666" data-file-height="2921" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Louis_Stevenson_sculpture_in_St._Giles%27_cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze relief memorial of Stevenson in St. Giles' Cathedral</div></div></div>
<p>A bronze relief memorial to Stevenson, designed by the American sculptor <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Saint-Gaudens" title="Augustus Saint-Gaudens">Augustus Saint-Gaudens</a> in 1904, is mounted in the Moray Aisle of <a href="/wiki/St_Giles%27_Cathedral" title="St Giles&#39; Cathedral">St Giles' Cathedral</a>, Edinburgh.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Saint-Gaudens' scaled-down version of this relief is in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Montclair_Art_Museum" title="Montclair Art Museum">Montclair Art Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Another small version depicting Stevenson with a cigarette in his hand rather than the pen he holds in the St. Giles memorial is displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Nichols_House_Museum" title="Nichols House Museum">Nichols House Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Beacon_Hill,_Boston" title="Beacon Hill, Boston">Beacon Hill, Boston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another memorial in Edinburgh stands in West Princes Street Gardens below <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle" title="Edinburgh Castle">Edinburgh Castle</a>; it is a simple upright stone inscribed with "RLS – A Man of Letters 1850–1894" by sculptor <a href="/wiki/Ian_Hamilton_Finlay" title="Ian Hamilton Finlay">Ian Hamilton Finlay</a> in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, a statue of Stevenson as a child with his dog was unveiled by the author <a href="/wiki/Ian_Rankin" title="Ian Rankin">Ian Rankin</a> outside <a href="/wiki/Colinton_Parish_Church" title="Colinton Parish Church">Colinton Parish Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCRLS_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCRLS-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The sculptor of the statue was Alan Herriot, and the money to erect it was raised by the Colinton Community Conservation Trust.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCRLS_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCRLS-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A plaque above the door of a house in <a href="/wiki/Braemar" title="Braemar">Castleton of Braemar</a> states "Here R.L. Stevenson spent the Summer of 1881 and wrote Treasure Island, his first great work".
</p><p>A garden was designed by the Bournemouth Corporation in 1957 as a memorial to Stevenson, on the site of his <a href="/wiki/Westbourne,_Dorset" title="Westbourne, Dorset">Westbourne</a> house, "Skerryvore", which he occupied from 1885 to 1887. A statue of the <a href="/wiki/Skerryvore" title="Skerryvore">Skerryvore</a> lighthouse is present on the site.
</p><p>In 1966, the Canadian actor <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Bochner" title="Lloyd Bochner">Lloyd Bochner</a> played Stevenson in the episode "Jolly Roger and Wells Fargo" of the <a href="/wiki/Television_syndication" class="mw-redirect" title="Television syndication">syndicated</a> American television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Death_Valley_Days" title="Death Valley Days">Death Valley Days</a></i>, hosted by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Taylor_(actor)" title="Robert Taylor (actor)">Robert Taylor</a> and directed by <a href="/wiki/Denver_Pyle" title="Denver Pyle">Denver Pyle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> In an earlier <i>Death Valley Days</i> episode from 1958, "The Great Amulet", hosted by <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Andrews" title="Stanley Andrews">Stanley Andrews</a>, the actor Don Reardon (died 2004) played the role of Stevenson. In the story line, Stevenson falls in love with Fanny Osbourne, played by Aline Towne (1919-1996), the mother of two children in a loveless marriage in San Francisco. The couple met in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> where Stevenson was recuperating from health issues and moved to San Francisco, where Stevenson worked tirelessly despite lingering health matters in the production of his large volume of literary works. "The Great Amulet" is revealed at the conclusion of the episode.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, to mark the 100th anniversary of Stevenson's death, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland#Banknotes" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a> issued a series of commemorative <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">£1 notes</a> which featured a quill pen and Stevenson's signature on the obverse, and Stevenson's face on the reverse side. Alongside Stevenson's portrait are scenes from some of his books and his house in Western Samoa.<sup id="cite_ref-commemorative_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commemorative-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Two million notes were issued, each with a serial number beginning "RLS". The first note to be printed was sent to Samoa in time for their centenary celebrations on 3 December 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At least six US elementary schools are named after Stevenson, in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_West_Side" title="Upper West Side">Upper West Side</a> of New York City,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Fridley,_Minnesota" title="Fridley, Minnesota">Fridley, Minnesota</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">Burbank, California</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> in Grandview Heights, Ohio (suburb of Columbus), in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, California,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Merritt_Island,_Florida" title="Merritt Island, Florida">Merritt Island, Florida</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> There is an R. L. Stevenson middle school in <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a>, Hawaii and in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena,_California" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Helena, California">Saint Helena, California</a>. <a href="/wiki/Stevenson_School" title="Stevenson School">Stevenson School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pebble_Beach,_California" title="Pebble Beach, California">Pebble Beach, California</a>, was established in 1952 and still exists as a college preparatory boarding school. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_State_Park" title="Robert Louis Stevenson State Park">Robert Louis Stevenson State Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Calistoga,_California" title="Calistoga, California">Calistoga, California</a>, contains the location where he and Fanny spent their honeymoon in 1880.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A street in Honolulu's Waikiki District, where Stevenson lived while in the Hawaiian Islands, was named after his Samoan moniker: Tusitala. This was also (until recently changed) the name of a restaurant on Comiston Road, Edinburgh on the route of a favourite walk that Stevenson often took to the village of Swanston in the Pentland Hills.
</p><p>In 2011, Robert Louis Stevenson's <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a> defending <a href="/wiki/Father_Damien" title="Father Damien">Father Damien</a> from Rev. Dr. <a href="/wiki/Charles_McEwen_Hyde" title="Charles McEwen Hyde">Charles McEwen Hyde</a> influenced the founding of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Saint_Damien_Advocates&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Saint Damien Advocates (page does not exist)">Saint Damien Advocates</a> in Hawaii.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/GR_70" title="GR 70">Chemin de Stevenson (GR 70)</a> is a popular long-distance footpath in France that approximately follows Stevenson's route as described in <i>Travels with a Donkey in the Cévennes</i>. There are numerous monuments and businesses named after him along the route, including a fountain in the town of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Jean-du-Gard" title="Saint-Jean-du-Gard">Saint-Jean-du-Gard</a> where Stevenson sold his donkey Modestine and took a stagecoach to <a href="/wiki/Al%C3%A8s" title="Alès">Alès</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-castle_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-castle-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson_Memorial" title="Robert Louis Stevenson Memorial">Robert Louis Stevenson Memorial</a> is an outdoor memorial in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Square" title="Portsmouth Square">Portsmouth Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>, California.
</p><p>A memorial by <a href="/wiki/Gutzon_Borglum" title="Gutzon Borglum">Gutzon Borglum</a> was unveiled, in 1915, at Baker Cottage, <a href="/wiki/Saranac_Lake,_New_York" title="Saranac Lake, New York">Saranac Lake, New York</a>.
</p>
